Problem: Given that $a > 0$, if $f(g(a)) = 8$, where $f(x) = x^2 + 8$ and $g(x) = x^2 - 4$, what is the value of $a$? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: 4 \boxed{4}

Reference Answer: First, we solve for $b = g(a)$. \begin{align*} f(g(a)) &= 8 \\ f(b) &= 8 \\ b^2 + 8 &= 8 \\ b^2 &= 0 \\ \end{align*} Therefore, $b = g(a) = 0.$ Now, we solve for $a$ using $g(a) = a^2 - 4 = 0.$ From this, $a = \pm 2.$ Since we are given that $a > 0$, our answer is $a = \boxed{2}.$

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: If a function $g(x_1, x_2, ..., x_n)$ is anti-symmetric in $x_2$, prove or disprove that the integral of $g$ over a region symmetric with respect to $x_2$ is equal to 0. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out if the integral of an anti-symmetric function \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) over a symmetric region with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero. Let me start by recalling what anti-symmetric means. If a function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), that means if we swap the variable \( x_2 \) with some other value, the function changes sign. Wait, actually, anti-symmetric usually refers to swapping two variables. Hmm, maybe I need to clarify. The problem states that \( g \) is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \). Hmm, anti-symmetric with respect to what? Maybe it's anti-symmetric when swapping \( x_2 \) with another variable, but since the region is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), maybe it's anti-symmetric under reflection in \( x_2 \). Let me think. If the function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), that might mean \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So reflecting \( x_2 \) to \(-x_2\) changes the sign. And the region over which we're integrating is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \). So the region would be unchanged if we reflect \( x_2 \) to \(-x_2 \). Okay, so if the region is symmetric in \( x_2 \), then for every point \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) in the region, the point \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is also in the region. And the function \( g \) at \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So when we integrate over the entire region, each point \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) has a corresponding point \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \), and their contributions to the integral are \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) and \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). But wait, when you integrate, you have to consider the measure. Let me make this precise. Suppose the region \( R \) is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), so if \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R \), then \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R \). Let me perform a change of variables in the integral \( I = \int_R g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \). Let’s perform the substitution \( x_2 \to -x_2 \). Let’s denote \( y_2 = -x_2 \), then \( dy_2 = -dx_2 \). The Jacobian determinant here is just -1, but since we're taking the absolute value for integration, the volume element remains the same. Wait, but actually, in one-variable substitution, when you do \( y = -x \), \( dy = -dx \), so the integral becomes \( \int_{y(a)}^{y(b)} f(-y) (-dy) ) \). But if the region is symmetric, then the limits for \( x_2 \) would be from -a to a, so when you substitute \( y_2 = -x_2 \), the limits become from a to -a, and then flipping the limits removes the negative sign. So effectively, the integral over \( x_2 \) from -a to a becomes an integral over \( y_2 \) from -a to a, but with the substitution. But given the function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), so \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So if I perform this substitution, the integral becomes: \( I = \int_R g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) After substitution \( x_2 \to -x_2 \), which leaves the region R unchanged (due to symmetry), the integral becomes: \( I = \int_R g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 (-dx_2) \ldots dx_n \) But the substitution flips the sign of \( dx_2 \), but since the region is symmetric, the limits of integration for \( x_2 \) go from some lower limit to upper limit, and after substitution, they reverse, which would introduce a negative sign. But since the region is symmetric, those limits are symmetric around zero, so substituting \( x_2 \to -x_2 \) just swaps the limits, but integrating from -a to a is the same as integrating from a to -a with a negative sign. So combining both the negative from the substitution and the negative from the limits, they cancel out. Wait, this is getting confusing. Let me try again. Alternatively, split the integral into two parts: one where \( x_2 \geq 0 \) and one where \( x_2 \leq 0 \). Let’s denote \( R_+ = \{ (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R | x_2 \geq 0 \} \) and \( R_- = \{ (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R | x_2 \leq 0 \} \). Because R is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), the region \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Therefore, the integral over R can be written as the integral over \( R_+ \) plus the integral over \( R_- \). Now, consider changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \): replace \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \). Then, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes an integral over \( R_+ \), but with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \). So, \( I = \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 (-dx_2) \ldots dx_n \) But since we are replacing \( x_2 \) by \( -x_2 \), the differential \( dx_2 \) becomes \( -dx_2 \). However, when we reverse the limits of integration, the negative sign cancels out. Since \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \), the transformed integral over \( R_- \) becomes the integral over \( R_+ \) with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \), and the measure becomes \( dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) again because the limits adjust to account for the reflection. So actually, the substitution introduces a factor of -1 from the function, but the measure remains the same. Therefore, the integral over \( R_- \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) But because \( g \) is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), this becomes: \( \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) Therefore, the total integral \( I \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g \, dV + \int_{R_+} -g \, dV = \int_{R_+} g - g \, dV = 0 \) Hence, the integral over the symmetric region of an anti-symmetric function is zero. So this seems to hold. Let me check with a simple example to verify. Take \( n = 1 \), but wait, anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), so maybe n=2. Let’s take \( g(x, y) = y \), which is anti-symmetric in y. Integrate over a symmetric region in y, say the rectangle \( [-1, 1] \times [-1, 1] \). The integral of y over this region would be the integral from x=-1 to 1, y=-1 to 1 of y dx dy. Integrating over y first, the integral of y from -1 to 1 is zero, since it's an odd function. So indeed, the integral is zero. Another example: 3D, integrate z over a sphere centered at the origin. The integral should be zero because z is anti-symmetric with respect to reflection over z, and the sphere is symmetric. Yep, that works. Alternatively, if the region wasn't symmetric, say integrating y over [0,1] x [0,1], then the integral would not be zero. So the symmetry of the region is essential. Therefore, the reasoning seems correct. By splitting the integral into two mirrored parts and using the anti-symmetry, the contributions cancel each other out. Therefore, the integral should be zero. So the statement is true. **Final Answer** \boxed{0} </think> To determine if the integral of an anti-symmetric function \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) over a region symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero, we start by noting that anti-symmetry in \( x_2 \) means \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). The region of integration is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), meaning if \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is in the region, then \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is also in the region. We split the integral into two parts: one where \( x_2 \geq 0 \) (denoted \( R_+ \)) and one where \( x_2 \leq 0 \) (denoted \( R_- \)). Due to the symmetry of the region, \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \) by replacing \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \), we transform the integral over \( R_- \) into an integral over \( R_+ \). This substitution changes \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) to \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \), which is \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) due to anti-symmetry. The measure remains the same because the region is symmetric. Thus, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes: \[ \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \] Combining the integrals over \( R_+ \) and \( R_- \): \[ \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n = 0 \] Therefore, the integral of an anti-symmetric function over a symmetric region with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero. \[ \boxed{0} \]

Wait, but actually, in one-variable substitution, when you do \( y = -x \), \( dy = -dx \), so the integral becomes \( \int_{y(a)}^{y(b)} f(-y) (-dy) ) \). But if the region is symmetric, then the limits for \( x_2 \) would be from -a to a, so when you substitute \( y_2 = -x_2 \), the limits become from a to -a, and then flipping the limits removes the negative sign. So effectively, the integral over \( x_2 \) from -a to a becomes an integral over \( y_2 \) from -a to a, but with the substitution. But given the function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), so \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So if I perform this substitution, the integral becomes: \( I = \int_R g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) After substitution \( x_2 \to -x_2 \), which leaves the region R unchanged (due to symmetry), the integral becomes: \( I = \int_R g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 (-dx_2) \ldots dx_n \) But the substitution flips the sign of \( dx_2 \), but since the region is symmetric, the limits of integration for \( x_2 \) go from some lower limit to upper limit, and after substitution, they reverse, which would introduce a negative sign. But since the region is symmetric, those limits are symmetric around zero, so substituting \( x_2 \to -x_2 \) just swaps the limits, but integrating from -a to a is the same as integrating from a to -a with a negative sign. So combining both the negative from the substitution and the negative from the limits, they cancel out. Wait, this is getting confusing. Let me try again. Alternatively, split the integral into two parts: one where \( x_2 \geq 0 \) and one where \( x_2 \leq 0 \). Let’s denote \( R_+ = \{ (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R | x_2 \geq 0 \} \) and \( R_- = \{ (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R | x_2 \leq 0 \} \). Because R is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), the region \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Therefore, the integral over R can be written as the integral over \( R_+ \) plus the integral over \( R_- \). Now, consider changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \): replace \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \). Then, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes an integral over \( R_+ \), but with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \). So, \( I = \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 (-dx_2) \ldots dx_n \) But since we are replacing \( x_2 \) by \( -x_2 \), the differential \( dx_2 \) becomes \( -dx_2 \). However, when we reverse the limits of integration, the negative sign cancels out. Since \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \), the transformed integral over \( R_- \) becomes the integral over \( R_+ \) with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \), and the measure becomes \( dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) again because the limits adjust to account for the reflection. So actually, the substitution introduces a factor of -1 from the function, but the measure remains the same. Therefore, the integral over \( R_- \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) But because \( g \) is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), this becomes: \( \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) Therefore, the total integral \( I \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g \, dV + \int_{R_+} -g \, dV = \int_{R_+} g - g \, dV = 0 \) Hence, the integral over the symmetric region of an anti-symmetric function is zero. So this seems to hold. Let me check with a simple example to verify. Take \( n = 1 \), but wait, anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), so maybe n=2. Let’s take \( g(x, y) = y \), which is anti-symmetric in y. Integrate over a symmetric region in y, say the rectangle \( [-1, 1] \times [-1, 1] \). The integral of y over this region would be the integral from x=-1 to 1, y=-1 to 1 of y dx dy. Integrating over y first, the integral of y from -1 to 1 is zero, since it's an odd function. So indeed, the integral is zero. Another example: 3D, integrate z over a sphere centered at the origin. The integral should be zero because z is anti-symmetric with respect to reflection over z, and the sphere is symmetric. Yep, that works. Alternatively, if the region wasn't symmetric, say integrating y over [0,1] x [0,1], then the integral would not be zero. So the symmetry of the region is essential. Therefore, the reasoning seems correct. By splitting the integral into two mirrored parts and using the anti-symmetry, the contributions cancel each other out. Therefore, the integral should be zero. So the statement is true. **Final Answer** \boxed{0} </think> To determine if the integral of an anti-symmetric function \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) over a region symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero, we start by noting that anti-symmetry in \( x_2 \) means \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). The region of integration is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), meaning if \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is in the region, then \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is also in the region. We split the integral into two parts: one where \( x_2 \geq 0 \) (denoted \( R_+ \)) and one where \( x_2 \leq 0 \) (denoted \( R_- \)). Due to the symmetry of the region, \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \) by replacing \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \), we transform the integral over \( R_- \) into an integral over \( R_+ \). This substitution changes \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) to \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \), which is \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) due to anti-symmetry. The measure remains the same because the region is symmetric. Thus, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes: \[ \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \] Combining the integrals over \( R_+ \) and \( R_- \): \[ \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n = 0 \] Therefore, the integral of an anti-symmetric function over a symmetric region with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero. \[ \boxed{0} \]

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out if the integral of an anti-symmetric function \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) over a symmetric region with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero. Let me start by recalling what anti-symmetric means. If a function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), that means if we swap the variable \( x_2 \) with some other value, the function changes sign. Wait, actually, anti-symmetric usually refers to swapping two variables. Hmm, maybe I need to clarify. The problem states that \( g \) is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \). Hmm, anti-symmetric with respect to what? Maybe it's anti-symmetric when swapping \( x_2 \) with another variable, but since the region is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), maybe it's anti-symmetric under reflection in \( x_2 \). Let me think. If the function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), that might mean \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So reflecting \( x_2 \) to \(-x_2\) changes the sign. And the region over which we're integrating is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \). So the region would be unchanged if we reflect \( x_2 \) to \(-x_2 \). Okay, so if the region is symmetric in \( x_2 \), then for every point \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) in the region, the point \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is also in the region. And the function \( g \) at \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So when we integrate over the entire region, each point \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) has a corresponding point \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \), and their contributions to the integral are \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) and \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). But wait, when you integrate, you have to consider the measure. Let me make this precise. Suppose the region \( R \) is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), so if \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R \), then \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R \). Let me perform a change of variables in the integral \( I = \int_R g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \). Let’s perform the substitution \( x_2 \to -x_2 \). Let’s denote \( y_2 = -x_2 \), then \( dy_2 = -dx_2 \). The Jacobian determinant here is just -1, but since we're taking the absolute value for integration, the volume element remains the same. Wait, but actually, in one-variable substitution, when you do \( y = -x \), \( dy = -dx \), so the integral becomes \( \int_{y(a)}^{y(b)} f(-y) (-dy) ) \). But if the region is symmetric, then the limits for \( x_2 \) would be from -a to a, so when you substitute \( y_2 = -x_2 \), the limits become from a to -a, and then flipping the limits removes the negative sign. So effectively, the integral over \( x_2 \) from -a to a becomes an integral over \( y_2 \) from -a to a, but with the substitution. But given the function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), so \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So if I perform this substitution, the integral becomes: \( I = \int_R g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) After substitution \( x_2 \to -x_2 \), which leaves the region R unchanged (due to symmetry), the integral becomes: \( I = \int_R g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 (-dx_2) \ldots dx_n \) But the substitution flips the sign of \( dx_2 \), but since the region is symmetric, the limits of integration for \( x_2 \) go from some lower limit to upper limit, and after substitution, they reverse, which would introduce a negative sign. But since the region is symmetric, those limits are symmetric around zero, so substituting \( x_2 \to -x_2 \) just swaps the limits, but integrating from -a to a is the same as integrating from a to -a with a negative sign. So combining both the negative from the substitution and the negative from the limits, they cancel out. Wait, this is getting confusing. Let me try again. Alternatively, split the integral into two parts: one where \( x_2 \geq 0 \) and one where \( x_2 \leq 0 \). Let’s denote \( R_+ = \{ (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R | x_2 \geq 0 \} \) and \( R_- = \{ (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R | x_2 \leq 0 \} \). Because R is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), the region \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Therefore, the integral over R can be written as the integral over \( R_+ \) plus the integral over \( R_- \). Now, consider changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \): replace \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \). Then, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes an integral over \( R_+ \), but with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \). So, \( I = \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 (-dx_2) \ldots dx_n \) But since we are replacing \( x_2 \) by \( -x_2 \), the differential \( dx_2 \) becomes \( -dx_2 \). However, when we reverse the limits of integration, the negative sign cancels out. Since \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \), the transformed integral over \( R_- \) becomes the integral over \( R_+ \) with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \), and the measure becomes \( dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) again because the limits adjust to account for the reflection. So actually, the substitution introduces a factor of -1 from the function, but the measure remains the same. Therefore, the integral over \( R_- \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) But because \( g \) is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), this becomes: \( \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) Therefore, the total integral \( I \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g \, dV + \int_{R_+} -g \, dV = \int_{R_+} g - g \, dV = 0 \) Hence, the integral over the symmetric region of an anti-symmetric function is zero. So this seems to hold. Let me check with a simple example to verify. Take \( n = 1 \), but wait, anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), so maybe n=2. Let’s take \( g(x, y) = y \), which is anti-symmetric in y. Integrate over a symmetric region in y, say the rectangle \( [-1, 1] \times [-1, 1] \). The integral of y over this region would be the integral from x=-1 to 1, y=-1 to 1 of y dx dy. Integrating over y first, the integral of y from -1 to 1 is zero, since it's an odd function. So indeed, the integral is zero. Another example: 3D, integrate z over a sphere centered at the origin. The integral should be zero because z is anti-symmetric with respect to reflection over z, and the sphere is symmetric. Yep, that works. Alternatively, if the region wasn't symmetric, say integrating y over [0,1] x [0,1], then the integral would not be zero. So the symmetry of the region is essential. Therefore, the reasoning seems correct. By splitting the integral into two mirrored parts and using the anti-symmetry, the contributions cancel each other out. Therefore, the integral should be zero. So the statement is true. **Final Answer** \boxed{0} </think> To determine if the integral of an anti-symmetric function \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) over a region symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero, we start by noting that anti-symmetry in \( x_2 \) means \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). The region of integration is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), meaning if \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is in the region, then \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is also in the region. We split the integral into two parts: one where \( x_2 \geq 0 \) (denoted \( R_+ \)) and one where \( x_2 \leq 0 \) (denoted \( R_- \)). Due to the symmetry of the region, \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \) by replacing \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \), we transform the integral over \( R_- \) into an integral over \( R_+ \). This substitution changes \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) to \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \), which is \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) due to anti-symmetry. The measure remains the same because the region is symmetric. Thus, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes: \[ \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \] Combining the integrals over \( R_+ \) and \( R_- \): \[ \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n = 0 \] Therefore, the integral of an anti-symmetric function over a symmetric region with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero. \[ \boxed{0} \]

Alternatively, if the region wasn't symmetric, say integrating y over [0,1] x [0,1], then the integral would not be zero. So the symmetry of the region is essential. Therefore, the reasoning seems correct. By splitting the integral into two mirrored parts and using the anti-symmetry, the contributions cancel each other out. Therefore, the integral should be zero. So the statement is true. **Final Answer** \boxed{0} </think> To determine if the integral of an anti-symmetric function \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) over a region symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero, we start by noting that anti-symmetry in \( x_2 \) means \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). The region of integration is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), meaning if \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is in the region, then \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is also in the region. We split the integral into two parts: one where \( x_2 \geq 0 \) (denoted \( R_+ \)) and one where \( x_2 \leq 0 \) (denoted \( R_- \)). Due to the symmetry of the region, \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \) by replacing \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \), we transform the integral over \( R_- \) into an integral over \( R_+ \). This substitution changes \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) to \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \), which is \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) due to anti-symmetry. The measure remains the same because the region is symmetric. Thus, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes: \[ \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \] Combining the integrals over \( R_+ \) and \( R_- \): \[ \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n = 0 \] Therefore, the integral of an anti-symmetric function over a symmetric region with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero. \[ \boxed{0} \]

Problem: If a function $g(x_1, x_2, ..., x_n)$ is anti-symmetric in $x_2$, prove or disprove that the integral of $g$ over a region symmetric with respect to $x_2$ is equal to 0. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Since $g$ is anti-symmetric, then integrating over a region symmetric with respect to $x_2$ is 0. \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out if the integral of an anti-symmetric function \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) over a symmetric region with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero. Let me start by recalling what anti-symmetric means. If a function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), that means if we swap the variable \( x_2 \) with some other value, the function changes sign. Wait, actually, anti-symmetric usually refers to swapping two variables. Hmm, maybe I need to clarify. The problem states that \( g \) is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \). Hmm, anti-symmetric with respect to what? Maybe it's anti-symmetric when swapping \( x_2 \) with another variable, but since the region is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), maybe it's anti-symmetric under reflection in \( x_2 \). Let me think. If the function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), that might mean \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So reflecting \( x_2 \) to \(-x_2\) changes the sign. And the region over which we're integrating is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \). So the region would be unchanged if we reflect \( x_2 \) to \(-x_2 \). Okay, so if the region is symmetric in \( x_2 \), then for every point \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) in the region, the point \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is also in the region. And the function \( g \) at \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So when we integrate over the entire region, each point \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) has a corresponding point \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \), and their contributions to the integral are \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) and \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). But wait, when you integrate, you have to consider the measure. Let me make this precise. Suppose the region \( R \) is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), so if \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R \), then \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R \). Let me perform a change of variables in the integral \( I = \int_R g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \). Let’s perform the substitution \( x_2 \to -x_2 \). Let’s denote \( y_2 = -x_2 \), then \( dy_2 = -dx_2 \). The Jacobian determinant here is just -1, but since we're taking the absolute value for integration, the volume element remains the same. Wait, but actually, in one-variable substitution, when you do \( y = -x \), \( dy = -dx \), so the integral becomes \( \int_{y(a)}^{y(b)} f(-y) (-dy) ) \). But if the region is symmetric, then the limits for \( x_2 \) would be from -a to a, so when you substitute \( y_2 = -x_2 \), the limits become from a to -a, and then flipping the limits removes the negative sign. So effectively, the integral over \( x_2 \) from -a to a becomes an integral over \( y_2 \) from -a to a, but with the substitution. But given the function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), so \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So if I perform this substitution, the integral becomes: \( I = \int_R g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) After substitution \( x_2 \to -x_2 \), which leaves the region R unchanged (due to symmetry), the integral becomes: \( I = \int_R g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 (-dx_2) \ldots dx_n \) But the substitution flips the sign of \( dx_2 \), but since the region is symmetric, the limits of integration for \( x_2 \) go from some lower limit to upper limit, and after substitution, they reverse, which would introduce a negative sign. But since the region is symmetric, those limits are symmetric around zero, so substituting \( x_2 \to -x_2 \) just swaps the limits, but integrating from -a to a is the same as integrating from a to -a with a negative sign. So combining both the negative from the substitution and the negative from the limits, they cancel out. Wait, this is getting confusing. Let me try again. Alternatively, split the integral into two parts: one where \( x_2 \geq 0 \) and one where \( x_2 \leq 0 \). Let’s denote \( R_+ = \{ (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R | x_2 \geq 0 \} \) and \( R_- = \{ (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R | x_2 \leq 0 \} \). Because R is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), the region \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Therefore, the integral over R can be written as the integral over \( R_+ \) plus the integral over \( R_- \). Now, consider changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \): replace \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \). Then, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes an integral over \( R_+ \), but with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \). So, \( I = \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 (-dx_2) \ldots dx_n \) But since we are replacing \( x_2 \) by \( -x_2 \), the differential \( dx_2 \) becomes \( -dx_2 \). However, when we reverse the limits of integration, the negative sign cancels out. Since \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \), the transformed integral over \( R_- \) becomes the integral over \( R_+ \) with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \), and the measure becomes \( dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) again because the limits adjust to account for the reflection. So actually, the substitution introduces a factor of -1 from the function, but the measure remains the same. Therefore, the integral over \( R_- \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) But because \( g \) is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), this becomes: \( \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) Therefore, the total integral \( I \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g \, dV + \int_{R_+} -g \, dV = \int_{R_+} g - g \, dV = 0 \) Hence, the integral over the symmetric region of an anti-symmetric function is zero. Because R is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), the region \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Therefore, the integral over R can be written as the integral over \( R_+ \) plus the integral over \( R_- \). Now, consider changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \): replace \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \). Then, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes an integral over \( R_+ \), but with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \). So, \( I = \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 (-dx_2) \ldots dx_n \) But since we are replacing \( x_2 \) by \( -x_2 \), the differential \( dx_2 \) becomes \( -dx_2 \). However, when we reverse the limits of integration, the negative sign cancels out. Since \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \), the transformed integral over \( R_- \) becomes the integral over \( R_+ \) with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \), and the measure becomes \( dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) again because the limits adjust to account for the reflection. So actually, the substitution introduces a factor of -1 from the function, but the measure remains the same. Therefore, the integral over \( R_- \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) But because \( g \) is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), this becomes: \( \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) Therefore, the total integral \( I \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g \, dV + \int_{R_+} -g \, dV = \int_{R_+} g - g \, dV = 0 \) Hence, the integral over the symmetric region of an anti-symmetric function is zero. So this seems to hold. Let me check with a simple example to verify. Take \( n = 1 \), but wait, anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), so maybe n=2. Let’s take \( g(x, y) = y \), which is anti-symmetric in y. Integrate over a symmetric region in y, say the rectangle \( [-1, 1] \times [-1, 1] \). The integral of y over this region would be the integral from x=-1 to 1, y=-1 to 1 of y dx dy. Integrating over y first, the integral of y from -1 to 1 is zero, since it's an odd function. So indeed, the integral is zero. Another example: 3D, integrate z over a sphere centered at the origin. The integral should be zero because z is anti-symmetric with respect to reflection over z, and the sphere is symmetric. Yep, that works. Alternatively, if the region wasn't symmetric, say integrating y over [0,1] x [0,1], then the integral would not be zero. So the symmetry of the region is essential. Therefore, the reasoning seems correct. By splitting the integral into two mirrored parts and using the anti-symmetry, the contributions cancel each other out. Therefore, the integral should be zero. So the statement is true. **Final Answer** \boxed{0} </think> To determine if the integral of an anti-symmetric function \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) over a region symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero, we start by noting that anti-symmetry in \( x_2 \) means \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). The region of integration is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), meaning if \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is in the region, then \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is also in the region. We split the integral into two parts: one where \( x_2 \geq 0 \) (denoted \( R_+ \)) and one where \( x_2 \leq 0 \) (denoted \( R_- \)). Due to the symmetry of the region, \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \) by replacing \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \), we transform the integral over \( R_- \) into an integral over \( R_+ \). This substitution changes \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) to \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \), which is \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) due to anti-symmetry. The measure remains the same because the region is symmetric. Thus, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes: \[ \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \] Combining the integrals over \( R_+ \) and \( R_- \): \[ \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n = 0 \] Therefore, the integral of an anti-symmetric function over a symmetric region with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero. \[ \boxed{0} \]

Let me perform a change of variables in the integral \( I = \int_R g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \). Let’s perform the substitution \( x_2 \to -x_2 \). Let’s denote \( y_2 = -x_2 \), then \( dy_2 = -dx_2 \). The Jacobian determinant here is just -1, but since we're taking the absolute value for integration, the volume element remains the same. Wait, but actually, in one-variable substitution, when you do \( y = -x \), \( dy = -dx \), so the integral becomes \( \int_{y(a)}^{y(b)} f(-y) (-dy) ) \). But if the region is symmetric, then the limits for \( x_2 \) would be from -a to a, so when you substitute \( y_2 = -x_2 \), the limits become from a to -a, and then flipping the limits removes the negative sign. So effectively, the integral over \( x_2 \) from -a to a becomes an integral over \( y_2 \) from -a to a, but with the substitution. But given the function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), so \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So if I perform this substitution, the integral becomes: \( I = \int_R g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) After substitution \( x_2 \to -x_2 \), which leaves the region R unchanged (due to symmetry), the integral becomes: \( I = \int_R g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 (-dx_2) \ldots dx_n \) But the substitution flips the sign of \( dx_2 \), but since the region is symmetric, the limits of integration for \( x_2 \) go from some lower limit to upper limit, and after substitution, they reverse, which would introduce a negative sign. But since the region is symmetric, those limits are symmetric around zero, so substituting \( x_2 \to -x_2 \) just swaps the limits, but integrating from -a to a is the same as integrating from a to -a with a negative sign. So combining both the negative from the substitution and the negative from the limits, they cancel out. Wait, this is getting confusing. Let me try again. Alternatively, split the integral into two parts: one where \( x_2 \geq 0 \) and one where \( x_2 \leq 0 \). Let’s denote \( R_+ = \{ (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R | x_2 \geq 0 \} \) and \( R_- = \{ (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R | x_2 \leq 0 \} \). Because R is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), the region \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Therefore, the integral over R can be written as the integral over \( R_+ \) plus the integral over \( R_- \). Now, consider changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \): replace \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \). Then, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes an integral over \( R_+ \), but with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \). So, \( I = \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 (-dx_2) \ldots dx_n \) But since we are replacing \( x_2 \) by \( -x_2 \), the differential \( dx_2 \) becomes \( -dx_2 \). However, when we reverse the limits of integration, the negative sign cancels out. Since \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \), the transformed integral over \( R_- \) becomes the integral over \( R_+ \) with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \), and the measure becomes \( dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) again because the limits adjust to account for the reflection. So actually, the substitution introduces a factor of -1 from the function, but the measure remains the same. Therefore, the integral over \( R_- \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) But because \( g \) is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), this becomes: \( \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) Therefore, the total integral \( I \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g \, dV + \int_{R_+} -g \, dV = \int_{R_+} g - g \, dV = 0 \) Hence, the integral over the symmetric region of an anti-symmetric function is zero. So this seems to hold. Let me check with a simple example to verify. Take \( n = 1 \), but wait, anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), so maybe n=2. Let’s take \( g(x, y) = y \), which is anti-symmetric in y. Integrate over a symmetric region in y, say the rectangle \( [-1, 1] \times [-1, 1] \). The integral of y over this region would be the integral from x=-1 to 1, y=-1 to 1 of y dx dy. Integrating over y first, the integral of y from -1 to 1 is zero, since it's an odd function. So indeed, the integral is zero. Another example: 3D, integrate z over a sphere centered at the origin. The integral should be zero because z is anti-symmetric with respect to reflection over z, and the sphere is symmetric. Yep, that works. Alternatively, if the region wasn't symmetric, say integrating y over [0,1] x [0,1], then the integral would not be zero. So the symmetry of the region is essential. Therefore, the reasoning seems correct. By splitting the integral into two mirrored parts and using the anti-symmetry, the contributions cancel each other out. Therefore, the integral should be zero. So the statement is true. **Final Answer** \boxed{0} </think> To determine if the integral of an anti-symmetric function \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) over a region symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero, we start by noting that anti-symmetry in \( x_2 \) means \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). The region of integration is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), meaning if \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is in the region, then \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is also in the region. We split the integral into two parts: one where \( x_2 \geq 0 \) (denoted \( R_+ \)) and one where \( x_2 \leq 0 \) (denoted \( R_- \)). Due to the symmetry of the region, \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \) by replacing \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \), we transform the integral over \( R_- \) into an integral over \( R_+ \). This substitution changes \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) to \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \), which is \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) due to anti-symmetry. The measure remains the same because the region is symmetric. Thus, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes: \[ \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \] Combining the integrals over \( R_+ \) and \( R_- \): \[ \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n = 0 \] Therefore, the integral of an anti-symmetric function over a symmetric region with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero. \[ \boxed{0} \]

Problem: If a function $g(x_1, x_2, ..., x_n)$ is anti-symmetric in $x_2$, prove or disprove that the integral of $g$ over a region symmetric with respect to $x_2$ is equal to 0. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: If $g$ is anti-symmetric in $x_2$, then $\int g(x_1, x_2,..., x_n) dx_2=0 \boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out if the integral of an anti-symmetric function \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) over a symmetric region with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero. Let me start by recalling what anti-symmetric means. If a function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), that means if we swap the variable \( x_2 \) with some other value, the function changes sign. Wait, actually, anti-symmetric usually refers to swapping two variables. Hmm, maybe I need to clarify. The problem states that \( g \) is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \). Hmm, anti-symmetric with respect to what? Maybe it's anti-symmetric when swapping \( x_2 \) with another variable, but since the region is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), maybe it's anti-symmetric under reflection in \( x_2 \). Let me think. If the function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), that might mean \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So reflecting \( x_2 \) to \(-x_2\) changes the sign. And the region over which we're integrating is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \). So the region would be unchanged if we reflect \( x_2 \) to \(-x_2 \). Okay, so if the region is symmetric in \( x_2 \), then for every point \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) in the region, the point \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is also in the region. And the function \( g \) at \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So when we integrate over the entire region, each point \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) has a corresponding point \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \), and their contributions to the integral are \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) and \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). But wait, when you integrate, you have to consider the measure. Let me make this precise. Suppose the region \( R \) is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), so if \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R \), then \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R \). Let me perform a change of variables in the integral \( I = \int_R g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \). Let’s perform the substitution \( x_2 \to -x_2 \). Let’s denote \( y_2 = -x_2 \), then \( dy_2 = -dx_2 \). The Jacobian determinant here is just -1, but since we're taking the absolute value for integration, the volume element remains the same. Wait, but actually, in one-variable substitution, when you do \( y = -x \), \( dy = -dx \), so the integral becomes \( \int_{y(a)}^{y(b)} f(-y) (-dy) ) \). But if the region is symmetric, then the limits for \( x_2 \) would be from -a to a, so when you substitute \( y_2 = -x_2 \), the limits become from a to -a, and then flipping the limits removes the negative sign. So effectively, the integral over \( x_2 \) from -a to a becomes an integral over \( y_2 \) from -a to a, but with the substitution. But given the function is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), so \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). So if I perform this substitution, the integral becomes: \( I = \int_R g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) After substitution \( x_2 \to -x_2 \), which leaves the region R unchanged (due to symmetry), the integral becomes: \( I = \int_R g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 (-dx_2) \ldots dx_n \) But the substitution flips the sign of \( dx_2 \), but since the region is symmetric, the limits of integration for \( x_2 \) go from some lower limit to upper limit, and after substitution, they reverse, which would introduce a negative sign. But since the region is symmetric, those limits are symmetric around zero, so substituting \( x_2 \to -x_2 \) just swaps the limits, but integrating from -a to a is the same as integrating from a to -a with a negative sign. So combining both the negative from the substitution and the negative from the limits, they cancel out. Wait, this is getting confusing. Let me try again. Alternatively, split the integral into two parts: one where \( x_2 \geq 0 \) and one where \( x_2 \leq 0 \). Let’s denote \( R_+ = \{ (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R | x_2 \geq 0 \} \) and \( R_- = \{ (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \in R | x_2 \leq 0 \} \). Because R is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), the region \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Therefore, the integral over R can be written as the integral over \( R_+ \) plus the integral over \( R_- \). Now, consider changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \): replace \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \). Then, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes an integral over \( R_+ \), but with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \). So, \( I = \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 (-dx_2) \ldots dx_n \) But since we are replacing \( x_2 \) by \( -x_2 \), the differential \( dx_2 \) becomes \( -dx_2 \). However, when we reverse the limits of integration, the negative sign cancels out. Since \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \), the transformed integral over \( R_- \) becomes the integral over \( R_+ \) with \( x_2 \) replaced by \( -x_2 \), and the measure becomes \( dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) again because the limits adjust to account for the reflection. So actually, the substitution introduces a factor of -1 from the function, but the measure remains the same. Therefore, the integral over \( R_- \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) But because \( g \) is anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), this becomes: \( \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \) Therefore, the total integral \( I \) is: \( \int_{R_+} g \, dV + \int_{R_+} -g \, dV = \int_{R_+} g - g \, dV = 0 \) Hence, the integral over the symmetric region of an anti-symmetric function is zero. So this seems to hold. Let me check with a simple example to verify. Take \( n = 1 \), but wait, anti-symmetric in \( x_2 \), so maybe n=2. Let’s take \( g(x, y) = y \), which is anti-symmetric in y. Integrate over a symmetric region in y, say the rectangle \( [-1, 1] \times [-1, 1] \). The integral of y over this region would be the integral from x=-1 to 1, y=-1 to 1 of y dx dy. Integrating over y first, the integral of y from -1 to 1 is zero, since it's an odd function. So indeed, the integral is zero. Another example: 3D, integrate z over a sphere centered at the origin. The integral should be zero because z is anti-symmetric with respect to reflection over z, and the sphere is symmetric. Yep, that works. Alternatively, if the region wasn't symmetric, say integrating y over [0,1] x [0,1], then the integral would not be zero. So the symmetry of the region is essential. Therefore, the reasoning seems correct. By splitting the integral into two mirrored parts and using the anti-symmetry, the contributions cancel each other out. Therefore, the integral should be zero. So the statement is true. **Final Answer** \boxed{0} </think> To determine if the integral of an anti-symmetric function \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) over a region symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero, we start by noting that anti-symmetry in \( x_2 \) means \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) = -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \). The region of integration is symmetric with respect to \( x_2 \), meaning if \( (x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is in the region, then \( (x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) is also in the region. We split the integral into two parts: one where \( x_2 \geq 0 \) (denoted \( R_+ \)) and one where \( x_2 \leq 0 \) (denoted \( R_- \)). Due to the symmetry of the region, \( R_- \) is the reflection of \( R_+ \) across \( x_2 = 0 \). Changing variables in the integral over \( R_- \) by replacing \( x_2 \) with \( -x_2 \), we transform the integral over \( R_- \) into an integral over \( R_+ \). This substitution changes \( g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) to \( g(x_1, -x_2, \ldots, x_n) \), which is \( -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \) due to anti-symmetry. The measure remains the same because the region is symmetric. Thus, the integral over \( R_- \) becomes: \[ \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n \] Combining the integrals over \( R_+ \) and \( R_- \): \[ \int_{R_+} g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n + \int_{R_+} -g(x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n) \, dx_1 dx_2 \ldots dx_n = 0 \] Therefore, the integral of an anti-symmetric function over a symmetric region with respect to \( x_2 \) is zero. \[ \boxed{0} \]

do_single_rollout

Problem: Sector $OAB$ is a quarter of a circle of radius 3 cm. A circle is drawn inside this sector, tangent at three points as shown. What is the number of centimeters in the radius of the inscribed circle? Express your answer in simplest radical form. [asy] import olympiad; import geometry; size(100); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8)); draw(Arc(origin,3,90,180)); draw((-3,0)--(origin)--(0,3)); real x = 3/(1 + sqrt(2)); draw(Circle((-x,x),x)); label("$B$",(0,3),N); label("$A$",(-3,0),W); label("$O$",(0,0),SE); label("3 cm",(0,0)--(-3,0),S); [/asy] Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Call the center of the inscribed circle $C$, and let $D$ be the point shared by arc $AB$ and the inscribed circle. Let $E$ and $F$ be the points where the inscribed circle is tangent to $OA$ and $OB$ respectively. Since angles $CEO$, $CFO$, and $EOF$ are all right angles, angle $FCE$ is a right angle as well. Therefore, the measure of angle $DCE$ is $(360-90)/2=135$ degrees. By symmetry, angles $ECO$ and $FCO$ are congruent, so each measures 45 degrees. Therefore, angle $DCO$ measures $135+45=180$ degrees, which implies $DC+CO=OD$. Also, $DC=r$, and $CO=r\sqrt{2}$, since triangle $CEO$ is an isosceles right triangle. Since $OD$ is a radius of the circle centered at $O$, we may set $DC+CO=r+r\sqrt{2}$ equal to 3 cm to find \[ r=\frac{3\text{ cm}}{\sqrt{2}+1}\cdot\frac{\sqrt{2}-1}{\sqrt{2}-1}=\boxed{3\sqrt{2}-3}\text{ centimeters}. \] [asy] import olympiad; import geometry; size(150); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8)); draw(Arc(origin,3,90,180)); draw((-3,0)--(origin)--(0,3)); real x = 3/(1 + sqrt(2)); draw(Circle((-x,x),x)); label("$B$",(0,3),N); label("$A$",(-3,0),W); label("$O$",(0,0),SE); draw((0,0)--(-3,0)); label("$C$",(-x,x),NE); label("$D$",(-3/sqrt(2),3/sqrt(2)),NW); label("$F$",(0,x),E); label("$E$",(-x,0),S); draw((-x,0)--(-x,x)--(0,x)); draw((-x,x)--(-3/sqrt(2),3/sqrt(2))); draw((-x,x)--origin,linetype("1 2"));[/asy]

Problem: Sector $OAB$ is a quarter of a circle of radius 3 cm. A circle is drawn inside this sector, tangent at three points as shown. [asy] import olympiad; import geometry; size(100); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8)); draw(Arc(origin,3,90,180)); draw((-3,0)--(origin)--(0,3)); real x = 3/(1 + sqrt(2)); draw(Circle((-x,x),x)); label("$B$",(0,3),N); label("$A$",(-3,0),W); label("$O$",(0,0),SE); label("3 cm",(0,0)--(-3,0),S); [/asy] Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{\sqrt{2}}

Reference Answer: Call the center of the inscribed circle $C$, and let $D$ be the point shared by arc $AB$ and the inscribed circle. Let $E$ and $F$ be the points where the inscribed circle is tangent to $OA$ and $OB$ respectively. Since angles $CEO$, $CFO$, and $EOF$ are all right angles, angle $FCE$ is a right angle as well. Therefore, the measure of angle $DCE$ is $(360-90)/2=135$ degrees. By symmetry, angles $ECO$ and $FCO$ are congruent, so each measures 45 degrees. Therefore, angle $DCO$ measures $135+45=180$ degrees, which implies $DC+CO=OD$. Also, $DC=r$, and $CO=r\sqrt{2}$, since triangle $CEO$ is an isosceles right triangle. Since $OD$ is a radius of the circle centered at $O$, we may set $DC+CO=r+r\sqrt{2}$ equal to 3 cm to find \[ r=\frac{3\text{ cm}}{\sqrt{2}+1}\cdot\frac{\sqrt{2}-1}{\sqrt{2}-1}=\boxed{3\sqrt{2}-3}\text{ centimeters}. \] [asy] import olympiad; import geometry; size(150); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8)); draw(Arc(origin,3,90,180)); draw((-3,0)--(origin)--(0,3)); real x = 3/(1 + sqrt(2)); draw(Circle((-x,x),x)); label("$B$",(0,3),N); label("$A$",(-3,0),W); label("$O$",(0,0),SE); draw((0,0)--(-3,0)); label("$C$",(-x,x),NE); label("$D$",(-3/sqrt(2),3/sqrt(2)),NW); label("$F$",(0,x),E); label("$E$",(-x,0),S); draw((-x,0)--(-x,x)--(0,x)); draw((-x,x)--(-3/sqrt(2),3/sqrt(2))); draw((-x,x)--origin,linetype("1 2"));[/asy]

Problem: Sector $OAB$ is a quarter of a circle of radius 3 cm. A circle is drawn inside this sector, tangent at three points as shown. What is the number of centimeters in the radius of the inscribed circle? Express your answer in simplest radical form. [asy] import olympiad; import geometry; size(100); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8)); draw(Arc(origin,3,90,180)); draw((-3,0)--(origin)--(0,3)); real x = 3/(1 + sqrt(2)); draw(Circle((-x,x),x)); label("$B$",(0,3),N); label("$A$",(-3,0),W); label("$O$",(0,0),SE); label("3 cm",(0,0)--(-3,0),S); [/asy] Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Call the center of the inscribed circle $C$, and let $D$ be the point shared by arc $AB$ and the inscribed circle. Let $E$ and $F$ be the points where the inscribed circle is tangent to $OA$ and $OB$ respectively. Since angles $CEO$, $CFO$, and $EOF$ are all right angles, angle $FCE$ is a right angle as well. Therefore, the measure of angle $DCE$ is $(360-90)/2=135$ degrees. By symmetry, angles $ECO$ and $FCO$ are congruent, so each measures 45 degrees. Therefore, angle $DCO$ measures $135+45=180$ degrees, which implies $DC+CO=OD$. Also, $DC=r$, and $CO=r\sqrt{2}$, since triangle $CEO$ is an isosceles right triangle. Since $OD$ is a radius of the circle centered at $O$, we may set $DC+CO=r+r\sqrt{2}$ equal to 3 cm to find \[ r=\frac{3\text{ cm}}{\sqrt{2}+1}\cdot\frac{\sqrt{2}-1}{\sqrt{2}-1}=\boxed{3\sqrt{2}-3}\text{ centimeters}. \] [asy] import olympiad; import geometry; size(150); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8)); draw(Arc(origin,3,90,180)); draw((-3,0)--(origin)--(0,3)); real x = 3/(1 + sqrt(2)); draw(Circle((-x,x),x)); label("$B$",(0,3),N); label("$A$",(-3,0),W); label("$O$",(0,0),SE); draw((0,0)--(-3,0)); label("$C$",(-x,x),NE); label("$D$",(-3/sqrt(2),3/sqrt(2)),NW); label("$F$",(0,x),E); label("$E$",(-x,0),S); draw((-x,0)--(-x,x)--(0,x)); draw((-x,x)--(-3/sqrt(2),3/sqrt(2))); draw((-x,x)--origin,linetype("1 2"));[/asy]

Since $OD$ is a radius of the circle centered at $O$, we may set $DC+CO=r+r\sqrt{2}$ equal to 3 cm to find \[ r=\frac{3\text{ cm}}{\sqrt{2}+1}\cdot\frac{\sqrt{2}-1}{\sqrt{2}-1}=\boxed{3\sqrt{2}-3}\text{ centimeters}. \] [asy] import olympiad; import geometry; size(150); defaultpen(linewidth(0.8)); draw(Arc(origin,3,90,180)); draw((-3,0)--(origin)--(0,3)); real x = 3/(1 + sqrt(2)); draw(Circle((-x,x),x)); label("$B$",(0,3),N); label("$A$",(-3,0),W); label("$O$",(0,0),SE); draw((0,0)--(-3,0)); label("$C$",(-x,x),NE); label("$D$",(-3/sqrt(2),3/sqrt(2)),NW); label("$F$",(0,x),E); label("$E$",(-x,0),S); draw((-x,0)--(-x,x)--(0,x)); draw((-x,x)--(-3/sqrt(2),3/sqrt(2))); draw((-x,x)--origin,linetype("1 2"));[/asy]

First, we let \( w = z + z^{-1} \). Using the identity for the cube of a sum, we have: \[ (z + z^{-1})^3 = z^3 + z^{-3} + 3(z + z^{-1}) \] This can be rewritten as: \[ w^3 = z^3 + z^{-3} + 3w \] Rearranging, we get: \[ z^3 + z^{-3} = w^3 - 3w \] Given the condition \( |z^3 + z^{-3}| \leq 2 \), we substitute to find: \[ |w^3 - 3w| \leq 2 \] We need to maximize \( |w| \) subject to \( |w^3 - 3w| \leq 2 \). Consider \( w \) as a real number. If \( w = 2 \cos \theta \), then using trigonometric identities, we find: \[ z^3 + z^{-3} = 2 \cos 3\theta \] The condition \( |2 \cos 3\theta| \leq 2 \) is always satisfied since \( |\cos 3\theta| \leq 1 \). The maximum value of \( |2 \cos \theta| \) is 2, achieved when \( \theta = 0 \) or \( \theta = \pi \), corresponding to \( z = 1 \) or \( z = -1 \). For complex \( w \), we consider the modulus. If \( w = 2 \), then: \[ |w^3 - 3w| = |8 - 6| = 2 \] which satisfies the condition. Testing values larger than 2 (e.g., \( w = 2.1 \)) results in \( |w^3 - 3w| > 2 \), violating the condition. Thus, the maximum possible value of \( |z + z^{-1}| \) is \( 2 \). \[ \boxed{A} \]

Using the identity for the cube of a sum, we have: \[ (z + z^{-1})^3 = z^3 + z^{-3} + 3(z + z^{-1}) \] This can be rewritten as: \[ w^3 = z^3 + z^{-3} + 3w \] Rearranging, we get: \[ z^3 + z^{-3} = w^3 - 3w \] Given the condition \( |z^3 + z^{-3}| \leq 2 \), we substitute to find: \[ |w^3 - 3w| \leq 2 \] We need to maximize \( |w| \) subject to \( |w^3 - 3w| \leq 2 \). Consider \( w \) as a real number. The maximum value of \( |2 \cos \theta| \) is 2, achieved when \( \theta = 0 \) or \( \theta = \pi \), corresponding to \( z = 1 \) or \( z = -1 \). For complex \( w \), we consider the modulus. If \( w = 2 \), then: \[ |w^3 - 3w| = |8 - 6| = 2 \] which satisfies the condition. Testing values larger than 2 (e.g., \( w = 2.1 \)) results in \( |w^3 - 3w| > 2 \), violating the condition. Thus, the maximum possible value of \( |z + z^{-1}| \) is \( 2 \). \[ \boxed{A} \]

If \( w = 2 \), then: \[ |w^3 - 3w| = |8 - 6| = 2 \] which satisfies the condition. Testing values larger than 2 (e.g., \( w = 2.1 \)) results in \( |w^3 - 3w| > 2 \), violating the condition. Thus, the maximum possible value of \( |z + z^{-1}| \) is \( 2 \). \[ \boxed{A} \]

If \( w = 2 \), then: \[ |w^3 - 3w| = |8 - 6| = 2 \] which satisfies the condition. Testing values larger than 2 (e.g., \( w = 2.1 \)) results in \( |w^3 - 3w| > 2 \), violating the condition. Thus, the maximum possible value of \( |z + z^{-1}| \) is \( 2 \). \[ \boxed{A} \]

Using the identity for the cube of a sum, we have: \[ (z + z^{-1})^3 = z^3 + z^{-3} + 3(z + z^{-1}) \] This can be rewritten as: \[ w^3 = z^3 + z^{-3} + 3w \] Rearranging, we get: \[ z^3 + z^{-3} = w^3 - 3w \] Given the condition \( |z^3 + z^{-3}| \leq 2 \), we substitute to find: \[ |w^3 - 3w| \leq 2 \] We need to maximize \( |w| \) subject to \( |w^3 - 3w| \leq 2 \). Consider \( w \) as a real number. If \( w = 2 \cos \theta \), then using trigonometric identities, we find: \[ z^3 + z^{-3} = 2 \cos 3\theta \] The condition \( |2 \cos 3\theta| \leq 2 \) is always satisfied since \( |\cos 3\theta| \leq 1 \). The maximum value of \( |2 \cos \theta| \) is 2, achieved when \( \theta = 0 \) or \( \theta = \pi \), corresponding to \( z = 1 \) or \( z = -1 \). For complex \( w \), we consider the modulus. If \( w = 2 \), then: \[ |w^3 - 3w| = |8 - 6| = 2 \] which satisfies the condition. Testing values larger than 2 (e.g., \( w = 2.1 \)) results in \( |w^3 - 3w| > 2 \), violating the condition. Thus, the maximum possible value of \( |z + z^{-1}| \) is \( 2 \). \[ \boxed{A} \]
